<div id="review_1343133006" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1343133006" />
    <a title="Gabrielle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6753400-gabrielle"><img alt="Gabrielle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598976963p2/6753400.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1343133006?book_show_action=true">Jul 23, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gabrielle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gabrielle" href="/user/show/6753400-gabrielle">Gabrielle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780062310095" href="/book/show/18090214-night-watch">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=movie-fodder">movie-fodder</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=ketchup">ketchup</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=russian-soviet">russian-soviet</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=vampires">vampires</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6753400-gabrielle?shelf=own-a-copy">own-a-copy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1343133006" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18410256153887012673">Russia is a fascinating country. Their history somehow manages to be just as grandiose as it is grim, and this influences their literature and art in very interesting ways. My experience of reading Russian literature has showed me that characters are very rarely all good or all evil. They are flawed, do good things for bad reasons and bad things for good reasons, struggle with very complex feelings and don’t often meet with a truly satisfying end.<br/><br/>Lukyanenko’s “Night Watch” is a collection of thr</span>
  <span id="freeText18410256153887012673" style="display:none">Russia is a fascinating country. Their history somehow manages to be just as grandiose as it is grim, and this influences their literature and art in very interesting ways. My experience of reading Russian literature has showed me that characters are very rarely all good or all evil. They are flawed, do good things for bad reasons and bad things for good reasons, struggle with very complex feelings and don’t often meet with a truly satisfying end.<br /><br />Lukyanenko’s “Night Watch” is a collection of three urban fantasy stories taking place in a post-USSR Moscow, when the entire country was starting to be disillusioned with their Westernization; those stories are rife with moral ambiguity, existential questioning, supernatural politics and a philosophy of good and evil that can most easily be translated as “good = thinks of others; bad = thinks of themselves”. Two Watches keep an eye over the city and maintain the Balance: the Night Watch are the Others (the once-human creatures with supernatural powers) of the Light, and the Day Watch are the Others of the Dark. Historically, their influence over humans has been the cause of many significant events, so they struck an uneasy peace a long time ago, to prevent a massive conflict that would have probably resulted in the apocalypse, and they have agreed on regulations about the use of their powers and influences to make sure that one side doesn’t have the upper hand on the other. <br /><br />So… supernatural beings who live by a highly regulated code and maintain an equilibrium of power based on the principle of thinking of the greater good. That sounds very Russian to me!<br /><br />“Night Watch” focuses on Anton, a rank and file Light magician, recently initiated as an Other, who still struggles with this new world he now lives in. He questions authority and the rules the Watches operate with because he feels that doing bad things for good reasons is not good enough. Basically, he still tries to make the world fit neatly into black and white categories while being confronted repeatedly with the murky gray of real life. In the first story, he must protect a young un-initiated Other, Egor, from a vampire, and figure out who cast an incredibly destructive spell over the lovely Dr. Svetlana, while he is going through this inner crisis. In the second story, a serial killer has been murdering Dark Others for three years, and suspicion falls on Anton, so he must prove his innocence by finding out who his responsible for these deaths. The final story of the collection is the least action-packed of the three: the members of the Watch are sent to get some well-deserved R&amp;R at someone’s country house, where they eat, get drunk and get very philosophical. That might sound weird and dull, but it actually brings the triptych to a very satisfying conclusion.<br /><br />If you have read any of my reviews, you know how much I love my anti-heroes, my flawed characters, my gritty settings and my complicated moral dilemmas. I loved sharing Anton’s moral confusion: as a newly initiated Other, he is the equivalent of a young teen: questioning the rules, getting upset when his “good” boss manipulates people just as much as the “bad” guys do (albeit with different motivation), trying to make everything clean, simple and un-ambivalent. His friendship with the young vampire Kostya is so interesting, because they are both so idealistic and green, and eventually become completely alienated when they realize that nothing is simple about keeping the Balance and that making friends who are on the other side (whatever that is) will never be smooth sailing.<br /><br />I also enjoyed sharing his realization that there is much more to his Watch colleagues than meets the eye as we get to know the supporting characters better. I am particularly fond of Olga and Semyon, both experienced Others: they are more cynical and jaded and their interaction with Anton clearly helps him grow and understand his reality and responsibilities better. The alternate-reality world-building is wonderful. The Watch’s universe is bleak, dirty and darkly atmospheric. The descriptions are minimal, the prose is clean but evocative. <br /><br />Having read the following three books in the World of Watches series (“Day Watch”, “Twilight Watch” and “Last Watch”), my only real criticism is that through the series, Lukyanenko uses the same structure and narrative tropes a lot. This means that over time, his stories become a little predictable. I still enjoyed all the books very much, so I supposed that if it ain’t broke, don’t fix it; but I still wish he could have pulled the rug out of under me a few times. I also think that his personal opinions, prejudices and philosophy bleed through the stories from time to time, which makes his prose feel occasionally didactic. We get it: you are a grumpy, Russian patriot with a sexist streak. I don’t let the author’s personal stuff disturb my enjoyment of the book, but it could definitely be grating to some readers.<br /><br />I recommend this great book (as well as the rest of the series) to fans of dark, urban fantasy and Russian literature.<br /><br />(The movie adaptation of “Night Watch” is essentially a loose adaptation of the first story. I think that without having read the book first, some of it might be confusing. The follow up movie “Day Watch” is not an adaptation of the second book, but a much looser interpretation of the second and third story of the first book. They have changed the story considerably: do not expect sequels, as they wrap up the story much too neatly. That being said, they are both fun movies and I enjoyed them very much – but less than the books!)</span>
  <a data-text-id="18410256153887012673" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_741371059" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/741371059" />
    <a title="David" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3335664-david"><img alt="David" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1551667298p2/3335664.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/741371059?book_show_action=true">Oct 14, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David" href="/user/show/3335664-david">David</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audible Audio " href="/book/show/12448490-the-night-watch">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Light magicians, Dark magicians, In Nomine players</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=fantasy-contemporary">fantasy-contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=urban-fantasy">urban-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=russian-literature">russian-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=1990s">1990s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=russia">russia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=supernatural">supernatural</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=witches">witches</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=moscow">moscow</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer741371059" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15266040918929633873">Some years ago, I wrote quite a lot of material for a roleplaying game called <i>In Nomine</i>. (That's why I have an Author page here on Goodreads, even though I'm not really an author, just a reader.) <i>In Nomine</i> was about the war between Heaven and Hell, and the players could take on the role of either angels or demons. The premise was that "they are much like us" - that is, angels and demons alike had similar feelings, doubts about the side they had chosen, and were both capable of good and evil, how</span>
  <span id="freeText15266040918929633873" style="display:none">Some years ago, I wrote quite a lot of material for a roleplaying game called <i>In Nomine</i>. (That's why I have an Author page here on Goodreads, even though I'm not really an author, just a reader.) <i>In Nomine</i> was about the war between Heaven and Hell, and the players could take on the role of either angels or demons. The premise was that "they are much like us" - that is, angels and demons alike had similar feelings, doubts about the side they had chosen, and were both capable of good and evil, however predisposed they were towards one or the other.<br /><br />It was meant to be a game about moral ambiguity and supernatural politics, though the execution was uneven, to say the least, and most games wound up being more like Book-of-Revelation-flavored superhero battles.<br /><br />I was reminded a lot of my old writing gig, and all those <i>In Nomine</i> supplements I still have sitting on my shelf, while reading <i>Night Watch</i>. Set in modern (late 90s, when it was written) Moscow, it's about two sides in an ancient battle of Good vs. Evil. The "Others" are beings of supernatural power born to human beings but fated to live among them and be conscripted into one side or the other. The Light is made up of those who have chosen to defend humanity, while the Dark is made up of those who use their powers for selfish ends and prey on humans.<br /><br />Except of course it isn't that simple. The Light and the Dark figured out years ago that if they ever really unleashed their powers on each other, the result would be an apocalypse that would destroy the world. So they formed a treaty that circumscribes what either side can do. In short, every interference in human affairs by one side authorizes an equal and opposite effect by the other. If a Light magician saves a life, a Dark magician gets to take one. If a Dark magician uses her powers for evil, the Light gets to use that much power for some good project. Over centuries, they have negotiated these rules and the terms under which each side may go about its business, and the result is a sort of detente (while each side hopes to someday gather enough power that they can actually win a final showdown).<br /><br />Naturally, both sides will cheat if they can get away with it. Each side is monitored by a "Watch" - the Light magicians are the Night Watch, because they watch what the Dark gets up to at night, while the Dark magicians of the Day Watch monitor the activities of the Light.<br /><br />Anton, the protagonist, in a book that's really a series of episodes (but continuous, so each affects the next) is a junior magician of the Light who comes up against the limits of his authority and what his side can do. He wants to do good and is continuously frustrated that even the smallest good deed means allowing the Dark to get away with something in exchange. He makes friends with a family of vampires, but has to remind himself that "legal" vampires just follow rules for hunting and killing humans to make sure they don't expose themselves or get carried away - they still hunt and kill humans.<br /><br />The magicians of the Dark aren't all mustache-twirlingly evil, and the magicians of the Light can be bastards, but they are still standing on opposite sides of a war.<br /><br />The moral ambiguity of <i>Night Watch</i> comes from how each side comes to terms with the accommodation they have made to keep things running smoothly. Their accommodation is called into question when, for example, an uninitiated magician of the Light, who knows nothing of the two sides or the ancient agreement, begins killing Dark magicians. Or when a child with great potential becomes a chip in the game, each side struggling to influence him, Anton's boss being no less devious and manipulative than his Dark counterpart.<br /><br />I liked the slow chess game being played out by the two sides - there aren't a lot of flashy magical pyrotechnics here, though there are some. The plot is more about moral quandaries and riddles of fate and destiny than who can win a supernatural throwdown.<br /><br />Anton is slightly flat as a character, but this book still had a great Moscow noir feel, and would have been good source material for some of those old <i>In Nomine</i> games.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15266040918929633873" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_60663244" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/60663244" />
    <a title="Brad" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1022982-brad"><img alt="Brad" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442484575p2/1022982.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/60663244?book_show_action=true">Jun 22, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brad" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brad" href="/user/show/1022982-brad">Brad</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781400025121" href="/book/show/1865653.The_Nightwatch">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=russian-lit">russian-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=urban-fantasy">urban-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=doing-the-dishes">doing-the-dishes</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=in-the-shower">in-the-shower</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=cutting-vegetables">cutting-vegetables</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1022982-brad?shelf=speculative">speculative</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer60663244" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11260613704691298563"><b>Destiny</b>: Around page two hundred I wondered whether Lukyanenko was going to throw us a <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/68494.Perdido_Street_Station__Bas_Lag_novel_' title='Perdido Street Station (Bas-Lag novel) by China Miéville' rel='nofollow noopener'>Perdido Street Station</a> style curveball and make <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1865653.The_Nightwatch' title='The Nightwatch by Sergei Lukyanenko' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Night Watch</a> about something other than a triple header search for an unsanctioned vampire, her young Other hostage, and the uber-powerful Warlock/Witch responsible for the great black Vortex hovering over the head of a nice, pretty little general practitioner (can you tell I've been reading too many mysteries and watching too much film noir lately? Sorry).<br/><br/>But</span>
  <span id="freeText11260613704691298563" style="display:none"><b>Destiny</b>: Around page two hundred I wondered whether Lukyanenko was going to throw us a <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/68494.Perdido_Street_Station__Bas_Lag_novel_" title="Perdido Street Station (Bas-Lag novel) by China Miéville" rel="nofollow noopener">Perdido Street Station</a> style curveball and make <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1865653.The_Nightwatch" title="The Nightwatch by Sergei Lukyanenko" rel="nofollow noopener">The Night Watch</a> about something other than a triple header search for an unsanctioned vampire, her young Other hostage, and the uber-powerful Warlock/Witch responsible for the great black Vortex hovering over the head of a nice, pretty little general practitioner (can you tell I've been reading too many mysteries and watching too much film noir lately? Sorry).<br /><br />But nope. It wrapped itself up quite nicely and satisfyingly. Our hero Anton Gorodetsky, a Wizard working for the Night Watch (the "good" guys) who is unsure of his powers, delivers an underdog victory against the forces of the Day Watch (the "bad" guys) and their bad ass leader, Zabulon. Anton manages to maintain the all important balance between good and evil (this struggle for balance is one of my favourite parts <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1865653.The_Nightwatch" title="The Nightwatch by Sergei Lukyanenko" rel="nofollow noopener">The Night Watch</a>, by the way), to save the boy, to save Doctor Svetlana and her untapped power (she is a seriously good ass wizard + she has a great name), and to rise in the estimation of his colleagues, despite being tricked into questioning the decisions of his boss, the toughest s.o.b. in Moscow, Boris Ignatievich.<br /><br />Then it ends. And that's when I turned the page to discover that <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1865653.The_Nightwatch" title="The Nightwatch by Sergei Lukyanenko" rel="nofollow noopener">The Night Watch</a> is really a compilation of three novellas. One down, two to go. Hope they're all as good as the first.<br /><br /><b>Among His Own Kind</b>: The second novella opens a few months later, and a serial killer named Maxim -- one of the Light -- has escaped detection over the course of his life, and he is busy slaying those of the Dark. He senses their evil, channels his good through a wooden toy dagger, and wipes out the souls of the Dark Ones with righteous fervor. <br /><br />The Night and Day Watches are then scrambling to put an end to Maxim's reign of terror as it threatens to tip the balance. Lukyanenko keeps us guessing who's really to blame, how Maxim's killings fit into the great chess game that is the Treaty, and the action drives on to yet another satisfying conclusion, but what this second tale is really about is the exploration of the concepts of good and evil from an Eastern European perspective. Neither good nor evil, you see, is about actions. Both the Light and the Dark engage in some pretty questionable behaviour -- murders, killings, betrayals, rule breaking, involuntary sacrifice -- but it is not these actions that make the difference between the Light and Dark in Lukyanenko's Russia; it is the choice between the individual and the group. <br /><br />The Dark Ones are evil because they believe in the individual. Their greatest selling point for new Others trying to find their way is their belief in absolute freedom. They can and do have happy loving families. They can love, grieve and care regardless of their selfishness, but they are evil because they care about themselves first and foremost. <br /><br />The Light Ones are good because they believe in the group. They believe in a greater good, and their individual needs and freedoms are second to the needs of everyone else (theoretically). And Lukyanenko, with all this talk about good and evil, makes sure we never lose sight of the balance between the two forces, which is necessary for peace. It's fascinating stuff, wrapped up and well concealed in an exciting urban fantasy. I can't help loving it. <br /><br /><b>All for My Own Kind</b>: And then it becomes a love story and my love for the book slips into mere appreciation. Although I feel more for Anton in the third episode of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/550720.The_Night_Watch" title="The Night Watch by Sarah Waters" rel="nofollow noopener">The Night Watch</a> and I am impressed by the further muddying of the ethical waters (the boundaries between the actions of the Light Magicians and Dark Magicians are practically non-existent), the final tale was too rushed to succeed. <br /><br />This part of the story could and should have been a novel all to itself. It is not long enough, and is, therefore, too rushed. I needed more time with Anton as he struggled with the direction of the Light, more time with Gesar and Olga (especially more about her background) and Svetlana to understand the decisions they were making and to develop some sustained suspense, more history of the Light's social experiments (Russian Revolution, Nazi Germany and others), more investment in the peripheral characters so that I cared for something beyond Anton and his philosophical struggles, and much much more of Zabulon and the Dark Ones.<br /><br />It's a bit of a let down after the genuine entertainment of the first two parts, but not such a let down that I will stop reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/32637.Sergei_Lukyanenko" title="Sergei Lukyanenko" rel="nofollow noopener">Lukyanenko</a>. Still, a couple of days ago I was planning to plow straight into <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/104160.Day_Watch__Watch___2_" title="Day Watch (Watch, #2) by Sergei Lukyanenko" rel="nofollow noopener">The Day Watch</a>, but now I think I'll wait until I have a long flight ahead of me. I bet it will make the perfect airplane book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11260613704691298563" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1522671213" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1522671213" />
    <a title="Deborah Ideiosepius" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4802663-deborah-ideiosepius"><img alt="Deborah Ideiosepius" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1425943228p2/4802663.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1522671213?book_show_action=true">Jan 23, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Deborah Ideiosepius" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Deborah Ideiosepius" href="/user/show/4802663-deborah-ideiosepius">Deborah Ideiosepius</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099489924" href="/book/show/1029770.The_Night_Watch">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4802663-deborah-ideiosepius?shelf=europe">europe</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4802663-deborah-ideiosepius?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4802663-deborah-ideiosepius?shelf=2016-reading-challenge">2016-reading-challenge</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1522671213" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3659637478139740751">In this, the first book of the night watch trilogy we follow Anton. Anton is a member of the Night Watch which means he is an 'Other' someone apparently human who dies in fact posses extraordinary powers which mean he is not, entirely, the same as other people. The others are divided into the 'Light ones' who do good and make up the Night watch in which they monitor the 'dark ones' who have their own 'Day Watch' and who do bad stuff. That is the basic premise of the novel.<br/><br/>I chose to read this be</span>
  <span id="freeText3659637478139740751" style="display:none">In this, the first book of the night watch trilogy we follow Anton. Anton is a member of the Night Watch which means he is an 'Other' someone apparently human who dies in fact posses extraordinary powers which mean he is not, entirely, the same as other people. The others are divided into the 'Light ones' who do good and make up the Night watch in which they monitor the 'dark ones' who have their own 'Day Watch' and who do bad stuff. That is the basic premise of the novel.<br /><br />I chose to read this because the movie was amazing, gritty, dark and uncompromising, I imagined that the book it was based on would also be amazing and to be honest it was pretty damn good! Very different from the movie though, not quite as dark and depressing, much more character development and some of the incidents are very different.<br /><br />The Night Watch is constructed as a series of three stories revolving around Anton, who is an Other and a member of the night watch. Without going into spoiler territory, the stories are convoluted political and clever. There are no straightforward good/evil themes here, no platitudes of how one side is better than the other and so it reads as both realistic and interesting.<br /><br />My main complaint about Night Watch would be that the rules that govern this 'verse are impossible to discern; if you do a bad deed then there has to be a good deed, but if none of the watches are there to see it how is it recorded? And why? At no point is the reason these watches exist well explained. It might well be, I suspect, that that is a plot element to the subsequent books, but at this point, trying to get through the first, there are times I wanted to throw in the trowel due to unexplained and unacknowledged plot inconsistencies. While it is true that in real life everything is not explained, here it is more annoying. I didn't buy real life.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3659637478139740751" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1438903664" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1438903664" />
    <a title="John Park" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17831138-john-park"><img alt="John Park" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1438903664?book_show_action=true">Nov 10, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John Park" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John Park" href="/user/show/17831138-john-park">John Park</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1438903664" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10999465837590862106">To begin with I'm out of sympathy with the basic idea. An elaborate parallel world of agents of the light and dark forces operating in a modern environment isn't the sort or urban fantasy that usually appeals to me. I prefer the secret quirk, the half-hidden monster, the dark hint of something other. For all its symbolic possibilities, Lukyanenko's scenario, with its enforced treaty between the two sides, its negotiations, power-struggles and rule-bendings, feels paradoxically mundane.<br/><br/>Inevitably</span>
  <span id="freeText10999465837590862106" style="display:none">To begin with I'm out of sympathy with the basic idea. An elaborate parallel world of agents of the light and dark forces operating in a modern environment isn't the sort or urban fantasy that usually appeals to me. I prefer the secret quirk, the half-hidden monster, the dark hint of something other. For all its symbolic possibilities, Lukyanenko's scenario, with its enforced treaty between the two sides, its negotiations, power-struggles and rule-bendings, feels paradoxically mundane.<br /><br />Inevitably he produces some good ideas and achieves some surprises. But somehow he or his translator, or the gap between my literary world and Lukyanenko's, has let a dead hand fall on this book, which for long stretches I found simply boring. Lukyanenko doesn't seem able to build tension. His first-person narrator goes off on tangential speculations or infodumps just when I want him to get out of the way and let the story advance. For much of the book the characters (whether undead or not) refused to come to life.<br /><br />Near the end, at a drinking party, several of the characters began to emerge from the miasma, to feel real and sympathetic. Perhaps significantly, this was when they were off the job, interacting with each other rather than being agents of the Light, briefly feeling things instead of talking about having feelings. The revelation of the Boss's motives would fit that description too, but it feels mishandled as a closing climax. I still have a nagging doubt as to whether I am bringing the wrong assumptions to this book, but it carries no editorial apparatus to help me.<br /><br />Lukyanenko does seem to have tied off most of the loose ends. Those that remain, such as Olga's offence and Alisher's quest for revenge, may be material for volume two. I don't expect to follow them there.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10999465837590862106" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ChOxVZmWnD60CkBNNx+cw5L8t7o8mqEdtABquc8ry4AwNdiHIshVVhMh9CzA6pYcRKNQcvIXL3rvfiwIAmg5Gg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('TvfdsUwX4TK7n6bf3rOpa6jxGpIwaHT26B5mSCdzvWR00bRj90koWhy0Er4pRqO0fq79Wv7l+pGzYCD56jBP/g==')})
  }

His works often feature intense action-packed plots, interwoven with the moral dilemma of keeping one's humanity while being strong.<br /><br />Lukyanenko is a prolific writer, releasing usually 1-2 books per year, as well as a number of a critical articles and short stories. Recently his works have been adapted into film productions, for which he wrote the screenplays. He lives in Moscow with his wife Sonia and two sons, Artemiy and Danil, keeps mice as pets and enjoys cooking.<br /></span>
